import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class MSARowSelfAttention(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads):
        super().__init__()
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.q = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.k = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.v = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.out = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)

    def forward(self, msa):
        B, S, N, D = msa.shape
        h, d = self.num_heads, self.head_dim
        x = self.norm(msa)
        q = self.q(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        k = self.k(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        v = self.v(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        out = (attn @ v).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).reshape(B, S, N, D)
        return self.out(out)


class OuterProductMeanSmall(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, hidden=32):
        super().__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.a = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)
        self.b = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)
        self.out = nn.Linear(hidden * hidden, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, msa):
        x = self.norm(msa)
        a = self.a(x)
        b = self.b(x)
        outer = torch.einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b)
        S = msa.shape[1]
        outer = outer / max(S, 1)
        B, N1, N2, H1, H2 = outer.shape
        return self.out(outer.reshape(B, N1, N2, H1 * H2))


class TriMulOut(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden):
        super().__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.pa = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.pb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.ga = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.gb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden)
        self.out = nn.Linear(hidden, pair_dim)
        self.gate_out = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, pair):
        x = self.norm(pair)
        a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x))
        b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x))
        o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b)
        o = self.out(self.norm_out(o))
        return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Full Evoformer block from AlphaFold2 combining MSA row self-attention,
    outer-product mean update, and triangular multiplicative update of the
    pair representation.  A single block of the iterative refinement stack.
    """

    def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, tri_hidden):
        super().__init__()
        self.msa_attn = MSARowSelfAttention(msa_dim, num_heads)
        self.opm = OuterProductMeanSmall(msa_dim, pair_dim)
        self.tri_mul = TriMulOut(pair_dim, tri_hidden)

    def forward(
        self, msa_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_repr: torch.Tensor
    ) -> tuple:
        """
        Args:
            msa_repr:  (B, S, N, msa_dim)
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
        Returns:
            (updated_msa, updated_pair)
        """
        msa_repr = msa_repr + self.msa_attn(msa_repr)
        pair_repr = pair_repr + self.opm(msa_repr)
        pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr)
        return msa_repr, pair_repr


msa_dim = 64
pair_dim = 64
num_heads = 4
tri_hidden = 32
seq_len = 32
msa_depth = 8
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    msa = torch.randn(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len, msa_dim)
    pair = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    return [msa, pair]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, tri_hidden]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation of the given PyTorch model. The model is a simplified Evaformer block with: 1. MSARowSelfAttention 2. OuterProductMeanSmall 3. TriMulOut We need to produce a class ModelNew with the same __init__ arguments and forward signature, producing numerically equivalent outputs, running faster. We have batch_size=2, msa_depth=8, seq_len=32, msa_dim=64, pair_dim=64, num_heads=4, tri_hidden=32. Since dimensions are relatively small (seq_len=32, msa_depth=8, batch=2), a custom fused CUDA kernel could be very beneficial because PyTorch's multiple separate ops have overhead. However, we need to be careful to get speedup. Fusing the entire forward pass into one or a few kernels will eliminate intermediate memory allocation and kernel launch overhead. We need to implement ModelNew. We can keep the nn.Linear, nn.LayerNorm layers as they are, because they are parameters. But we could also fuse them into the kernel, but then we need to access weights and biases. The easiest is to keep the submodules (or reference them) but compute the forward in a custom kernel using the parameters. However, PyTorch's nn.Linear and LayerNorm are implemented efficiently in cuBLAS/cuDNN. For small sizes, though, the overhead of multiple separate kernels is high. We might want to fuse the MSA attention, OPM, and tri_mul into one or a few kernels. But writing a fully fused kernel for attention on small sizes might be tricky. Alternatively, we can keep some PyTorch operations for the linear projections and launch custom kernels for the rest. But the evaluation might compare speed of the reference (which uses PyTorch operations) vs our custom kernel. Since the dimensions are small, the reference likely spends a lot of time on kernel launch overhead and memory transfers. If we fuse operations, we can reduce overhead. Potentially the biggest win is to fuse the OuterProductMeanSmall and TriMulOut, because they involve many small operations (einsum, reshape, linear). Actually, MSARowSelfAttention also has many steps: LayerNorm, 3 linears, reshape, transpose, batched matmul, softmax, matmul, reshape, linear. We could fuse the entire attention into a single kernel or keep linears in PyTorch and fuse the QK^T, softmax, and attention value into one kernel. Given the constraints and typical optimization patterns, fusing the entire forward pass into one or two custom CUDA kernels would be ideal. But we need to be careful about correctness and memory usage. Let's think about the operations step by step. ### MSARowSelfAttention Input msa: (B, S, N, D) where D = msa_dim. 1. x = LayerNorm(msa) 2. q = linear(x) -> (B, S, N, D) 3. k = linear(x) -> (B, S, N, D) 4. v = linear(x) -> (B, S, N, D) 5. reshape to (B, S, N, h, d) and permute to (B, S, h, N, d) Actually code: .view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) -> shape (B, S, h, N, d) 6. q @ k.transpose(-1, -2): (B, S, h, N, d) @ (B, S, h, d, N) -> (B, S, h, N, N) 7. Divide by sqrt(d) 8. Softmax over last dim 9. attn @ v: (B, S, h, N, N) @ (B, S, h, N, d) -> (B, S, h, N, d) 10. Permute back: .permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) -> (B, S, N, h, d) 11. Reshape to (B, S, N, D) 12. out = linear(out) Then msa = msa + out. ### OuterProductMeanSmall Input msa (same updated msa from previous step? Actually original msa after adding attention): Wait, in the Model.forward: msa_repr = msa_repr + self.msa_attn(msa_repr); pair_repr = pair_repr + self.opm(msa_repr); pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr). So opm takes the updated msa_repr (after residual addition). 1. x = LayerNorm(msa) -> (B,S,N,D) 2. a = linear_a(x) -> (B,S,N,hidden) 3. b = linear_b(x) -> (B,S,N,hidden) 4. outer = einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b) So for each batch, we sum over S: sum_s a[b,s,i,h] * b[b,s,j,k]. Actually einsum over 's' implicitly sums because 's' appears in both inputs but not in output. Let's check: "bsih,bsjk->bijhk". Yes, sum over s. So outer[b,i,j,h,k] = sum_s a[b,s,i,h] * b[b,s,j,k]. 5. Divide by max(S,1). S = msa_depth. 6. Reshape outer to (B, N, N, hidden*hidden) 7. out = linear_out(outer) -> (B, N, N, pair_dim) Then pair = pair + out. ### TriMulOut Input pair (after residual from opm). 1. x = LayerNorm(pair) -> (B,N,N,pair_dim) 2. a = pa(x) * sigmoid(ga(x)) -> (B,N,N,hidden) 3. b = pb(x) * sigmoid(gb(x)) -> (B,N,N,hidden) 4. o = einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) So o[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h]. Sum over k (seq_len dimension). 5. out = linear_out(norm_out(o)) -> (B,N,N,pair_dim) 6. gate = sigmoid(gate_out(pair)) -> (B,N,N,pair_dim) 7. result = gate * out Then pair = pair + result. ### Strategy Since sequence length is 32, MSA depth is 8, hidden sizes are small (32, 64). We can launch a kernel per block or per sample. Or fuse everything into a single kernel per batch element. But we also need to handle the linear layers. We could call the linear projects in PyTorch (which are fast) and then launch a custom kernel for the non-linear parts, fusing multiple operations. However, the linear layers themselves might be the bottleneck and we might want to fuse them too. But implementing matrix multiplication for small matrices in CUDA might not beat cuBLAS. The overhead of launching separate kernels for linear layers is small compared to the overhead of many other small operations (reshape, einsum, etc.). Actually PyTorch's linear layer calls a GEMM kernel which is highly optimized. But the shapes are small: for MSA attention, Q, K, V projections operate on (B*S*N, D) x (D, D) where B*S*N = 2*8*32 = 512, D=64. That's a 512x64 * 64x64 GEMM, which is small but okay. However, the einsum in OPM is (B, S, N, H) and (B, S, N, H) -> (B, N, N, H, H). That's a reduction over S (size 8) and outer product over H and H. If we do this with PyTorch, it might be implemented via a batched GEMM or explicit loops. The einsum in TriMulOut is (B,N,N,H) and (B,N,N,H) -> (B,N,N,H) with reduction over N (size 32). This is a batched matmul over N dimension. The key to speedup is likely to fuse operations to avoid intermediate memory and kernel launches. We can keep the linear projections in PyTorch but fuse the attention score computation, softmax, and output into one kernel. Similarly fuse OPM's einsum + scale + linear into one kernel. Fuse tri_mul's gate, a, b, einsum, norm, linear, gate_out, sigmoid, multiply into one kernel. Alternatively, we can fuse the entire forward pass into two kernels: one for MSA attention and one for the pair updates. But we need to get the weights from the nn.Linear modules. Since ModelNew must accept same constructor args and implement same forward signature, we can instantiate the same submodules inside ModelNew (or reuse them). But wait: the reference Model instantiates MSARowSelfAttention, OuterProductMeanSmall, TriMulOut. Our ModelNew can also instantiate these, but then compute forward using custom CUDA kernels that access the weights. Or we can keep the submodules for parameter storage and use them in custom kernels. We need to be careful: the evaluation will instantiate our ModelNew and call forward. If we keep the submodules, we need to make sure the parameters are initialized in the same way (well, they will be randomly initialized each time but the weights exist). The easiest is to replicate the architecture inside ModelNew's __init__ exactly as Model, storing the same layers. Then in forward, we can call our custom CUDA kernel using the parameters. But the evaluation also checks correctness. As long as the output matches, it's fine. However, we need to be careful about LayerNorm. LayerNorm has parameters gamma (weight) and beta (bias). We can use the stored nn.LayerNorm and call them in PyTorch, or we can fuse them into the kernel. Fusing them is nice. Given the overhead of multiple operations, a fully fused kernel for each major part is best. But maybe even a single kernel that does everything? That would be huge. Two or three kernels is manageable. Let's think about the kernels. #### Kernel 1: MSA Attention Inputs: msa (B, S, N, D) Inside kernel, for each location (b, s, n, d): - Compute x = layer_norm(msa[b,s,n,:]) using the stored weight and bias. - Compute q, k, v = x @ W_q_T + b_q, etc. But the linear layers have no bias in the reference for q, k, v; out has bias. - W_q shape (D, D). q = x * W_q (matrix-vector). Since D=64, we can have each thread handle one head or one element. Actually for attention, we need all N positions for Q, K, V to compute QK^T. So the kernel should process all (b,s,head,n) for the entire MSA. Given B=2, S=8, h=4, N=32, d=16 (since D=64, h=4 -> d=16). The attention matrix per (b,s,h) is N x N = 32x32. We could have each block handle one (b, s, h) and compute the attention for all N query positions. That would be 2*8*4 = 64 blocks. Each block computes Q (Nxd), K (Nxd), V (Nxd) for that (b,s,h). But Q, K, V depend on the full MSA after LayerNorm and linear projection. Since x depends on all n for a given (b,s), we could compute x = layer_norm(msa[b,s,:,:]) first. However, LayerNorm across D dimension for each (b,s,n) is independent. Wait, the LayerNorm in MSARowSelfAttention is over the last dimension D for each (b,s,n). So it's per-token. So for each (b,s,n), we compute layer norm of msa[b,s,n,:] -> x[(b,s,n)] of size D. Then we compute q, k, v = x @ W_q_T, x @ W_k_T, x @ W_v_T. W are shape (D,D). Since there is no bias. Then we need to compute for each head and each query: attn = softmax( (q[query] @ k^T) / sqrt(d) ). Then out = attn @ v. Then we need to reshape back and apply final linear projection. One approach: Use PyTorch to compute the linear projections and reshape into q, k, v. Then launch a custom kernel that does the batched attention and the final output linear. But the PyTorch linear projection involves a GEMM of shape (B*S*N, D) x (D, D) for each of q,k,v. That's 3 small GEMMs. We could also fuse those into the kernel. Better: In the kernel, we can compute q,k,v on the fly. For each (b,s,h), we need all n's x vectors. We can load the msa[b,s,n,:] into shared memory, compute LayerNorm, then compute q,k,v for all n. But implementing full GEMM in shared memory for D=64 is okay. Each W matrix is 64x64. We can store W_q, W_k, W_v in constant memory or shared memory, but since D is small, we can read them from global memory with coalescing. Actually we can have each thread handle one (n, head, qkv) element. Alternatively, we can do the linear projections with PyTorch using `einsum` or `matmul`, which are fast, and then launch a fused kernel for QK^T + softmax + attn @ v + reshape + out_linear. Let's think about which path gives the biggest speedup. Reference steps for MSA attention: 1. LayerNorm (elementwise kernel) 2. linear q (GEMM kernel) 3. linear k (GEMM) 4. linear v (GEMM) 5. reshape, permute (view, no kernel) 6. bmm q @ k^T (batched matmul kernel) 7. scale (elementwise) 8. softmax (fused softmax kernel) 9. bmm attn @ v (batched matmul) 10. permute, reshape (view) 11. linear out (GEMM) 12. add residual (elementwise) That's ~8 actual kernel launches (plus some small ones for views). A fused kernel could combine steps 5-12 into one kernel, or 1-12. But the overhead of 8 kernels for B=2, S=8, N=32 is significant. Implementing a kernel that does everything from LayerNorm to residual addition for attention seems feasible. However, we also have OuterProductMean and TriMulOut. Let's consider fusing them into the same kernel or separate kernels. If we launch 3 fused kernels, the overhead is still much lower than the reference. But perhaps the easiest to implement correctly is: **Fused kernel for MSA attention + residual:** - Compute Q,K,V linear projections and attention. - Input: msa, msa_weights (W_q, W_k, W_v, W_out), msa_biases (b_q=0, b_k=0, b_v=0, b_out), ln_weight, ln_bias. - Output: msa_updated = msa + out. **Fused kernel for OPM + pair residual:** - Input: msa_updated, pair, W_a, W_b, W_out, biases, ln_weight, ln_bias. - Output: pair_updated = pair + out. **Fused kernel for TriMulOut + pair residual:** - Input: pair_updated, pair_original? Actually pair_updated after OPM residual is input to tri_mul. Then result = pair_updated + tri_out(pair_updated). - So kernel does: compute a,b,gate, einsum, etc. But maybe we can combine OPM and TriMulOut into one kernel? That's tricky because TriMulOut depends on the updated pair after OPM. But it reads pair_updated. So we need to compute OPM first, then TriMulOut. So at least two kernels for pair branch. We can have: - Kernel 1: MSA attention. - Kernel 2: OPM. - Kernel 3: TriMulOut. Wait, the MSA attention includes batched matmuls over many dimensions. In PyTorch, `q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)` with shape (B,S,h,N,d) @ (B,S,h,d,N) is done via `torch.matmul` which expands to many batches. For small N=32, it's not huge. But the overhead might still be noticeable. Perhaps the biggest win is fusing the OPM and TriMulOut. Let's look at their PyTorch implementations. OPM: - LayerNorm (elementwise) - linear a (GEMM B*S*N x D -> B*S*N x hidden) - linear b (same) - einsum: sum over S of outer product over hidden. This is not a standard GEMM. PyTorch likely implements it with a custom contraction algorithm. It might create a large intermediate or use `tensordot`. The shape is (B,N,N,H,H). For B=2, N=32, H=32, that's 2*32*32*32*32 = ~2M elements. Each is 4 bytes = 8 MB. Not huge. - Divide by S. - reshape to (B,N,N,H*H) - linear out (GEMM B*N*N x H*H -> B*N*N x pair_dim). That's 2*32*32=2048 x 1024 -> 2048 x 64. Very small GEMM. TriMulOut: - LayerNorm (elementwise) - pa, pb, ga, gb: 4 GEMMs of shape 2048x64 -> 2048x32. - sigmoid (elementwise) - multiply a = pa * sigmoid(ga) (elementwise) - multiply b = pb * sigmoid(gb) (elementwise) - einsum "bikh,bjkh->bijh": This is sum over k of a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h]. For B=2, N=32, H=32. That's like a batched matrix multiplication where the batch is (b,h), and matrices are a_per_h of shape (N,N) and b_per_h of shape (N,N), but with transpose? Let's define A of shape (B,H,N,N) where A[b,h,i,k] = a[b,i,k,h]. B of shape (B,H,N,N) where B[b,h,j,k] = b[b,j,k,h]. Then einsum is A @ B^T? Actually sum over k: a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h] = sum_k A[b,h,i,k] * B[b,h,j,k] = (A * B) then sum over k? Wait, if B is indexed [b,h,j,k], then A[b,h,i,k] * B[b,h,j,k] summed over k is matrix multiplication A @ B^T if B is (j,k). But B is same orientation as A. So o[b,h,i,j] = sum_k A[b,h,i,k] * B[b,h,j,k]. That's like A * B^T (matmul) if B is transposed. But B^T would be (k,j). So it's A @ B^T where B has shape (j,k). Actually in standard matmul: (i,k) @ (k,j) -> (i,j). Here we have (i,k) @ (j,k) so we need to transpose second: (i,k) @ (k,j) if we transpose B from (j,k) to (k,j). So it's `torch.bmm(A, B.transpose(-1,-2))`. Where A and B are reshaped to (B*H, N, N). Let's verify: a shape (B,N,N,H). Permute to (B,H,N,N). b shape (B,N,N,H). Permute to (B,H,N,N). Then bmm(A, b.transpose(-1,-2)) yields (B*H, N, N). Then permute back to (B,N,N,H). That matches. - norm_out, linear_out: LayerNorm + GEMM 2048x32 -> 2048x64. - gate_out: linear 2048x64 -> 2048x64 + sigmoid. - multiply gate * out. So TriMulOut is also many small operations. Given the small sizes, writing a custom kernel for each of these fused blocks should yield a good speedup. But implementing all this in raw CUDA is a lot of code. Let's design the kernels carefully. ### Kernel 1: MSA Attention Input: - `msa` (B, S, N, D) float32 - `weight_ln` (D) - `bias_ln` (D) - `W_q` (D, D) - `W_k` (D, D) - `W_v` (D, D) - `W_out` (D, D) - `b_out` (D) Output: `msa_out` (B, S, N, D) = msa + attention(msa) Since B, S are small (2 and 8), we can have each block process one (b, s). Then within the block, we have N * D threads or similar. But attention for all heads and all N. Let's think of one block per (b, s, h). That would be 64 blocks. In each block: - Load msa[b, s, n, :] for all n into shared memory. Size N * D = 32*64 = 2048 floats = 8 KB. - Compute LayerNorm per n: load row, compute mean and variance, normalize, scale and shift. Store normalized x in shared memory (SMEM). That's another 8 KB. - Then compute Q, K, V for all n. For each head h, we need q_n = x_n @ W_q[h*d:(h+1)*d, :] ? Wait, W_q is (D, D). We can partition the threads to compute q, k, v. - Since d = D/h = 16. For a given head h, the q vector for each n is of size d. We can compute it by loading the corresponding columns of W_q. Actually W_q is stored transposed? In PyTorch linear, y = x @ W^T. So if W_q is (D, D), then q = x @ W_q^T. In memory, W_q is column-major? PyTorch stores tensors as row-major? Actually PyTorch uses NCHW? For 2D tensors, storage is (out_features, in_features). So W_q[i,j] corresponds to weight from input j to output i. The linear operation is y_i = sum_j x_j * W_q[i,j]. This is equivalent to a matrix multiplication where W_q is not transposed if we treat x as row vector and W as (out,in). In CUDA, we can compute q[b,s,n,:] = dot(x, W_q[:, :]) with W_q shape (D_out, D_in). Given that D is small, we can store W_q, W_k, W_v in shared memory as well? W is 64*64 = 4096 floats = 16KB. Three of them is 48KB. For N=32, x shared is 8KB. q,k,v shared: each is N*d = 32*16=512 floats = 2KB. Three is 6KB. attn matrix is N*N=1024 floats = 4KB. Total shared memory per block might be okay if we have at least ~80KB shared memory per SM. Let's check GPU specs. We need to call get_gpu_specs. Let's do that first.

Oh, and looking at PyTorch code for `OuterProductMeanSmall`: ```python def forward(self, msa): x = self.norm(msa) a = self.a(x) # (B, S, N, H) b = self.b(x) # (B, S, N, H) outer = torch.einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b) S = msa.shape[1] outer = outer / max(S, 1) ... ``` Wait, the einsum is over i and j (the N dimension). If I can do it in 1-2 kernels, great. ### Design for OPM: **Kernel 1**: Process all tokens to compute a and b with LN and projections. - Grid: B*S*N blocks, or B*S blocks with N threads each - Block: N=32 threads - Each block handles one (b, s), and its 32 threads handle i=0..31 - Each thread i: load msa[b,s,i,:], compute LN, compute a[b,s,i,:] = LN(msa) @ Wa, b[b,s,i,:] = LN(msa) @ Wb - Store a and b to global memory Actually, we could also use bmm or group matmul. But let's write the kernel. With 32 threads per block, each thread computes one token's a and b. a = 64-dim vector @ 64x32 matrix. Each thread computes 32 outputs, each is a 64-dot product. 32 * 64 = 2048 mul-adds per thread. For B*S = 16 blocks: total 16 * 32 * 2048 * 2 = 2M mul-adds. Trivial. Shared memory for X: each thread loads its own 64 floats into registers. **Kernel 2**: Einsum + linear + residual for OPM. - Grid: B * N * N = 2048 blocks - Each block handles (b, i, j) - Block size: let's say 256 threads - Phase 1: load a[b,:,i,:] (8x32) and b[b,:,j,:] (8x32) into SMEM - Phase 2: each thread computes 4 elements of outer (1024 / 256 = 4) But outer requires sum over s. If I assign each thread 4 elements, it needs to sum over s=8 for each. outer[h1, h2] = sum_s a[s, h1] * b[s, h2] / S With a[s,h] and b[s,h] in SMEM, each thread can compute its assigned elements. - Phase 3: compute linear on outer, add bias, add residual pair_out[b,i,j,d] = pair_in[b,i,j,d] + b_out[d] + sum_{h1,h2} outer[h1,h2] * W_out[h1*H+h2, d] For phase 3 with 256 threads: We have outer[1024] in SMEM (or registers, but we need all threads to access it). We want 64 outputs. One approach: use 64 threads to compute the 64 outputs, 192 threads idle. Or better, we can have all 256 threads participate: - Each thread computes partial sums for output[d] for a subset of d. - For example, thread t handles output[t % 64], and computes sum over k = t/64 to 1024 step 16. Actually, 256/64 = 4, so each output gets 4 threads. Thread group d (4 threads) collaboratively compute output[d]. Thread d*4 + i handles k starting at i, step 4: k = i, i+4, i+8, ... up to 1023. Each computes partial[d] += outer[k] * W_out[k, d] Then __shfl_down_sync within the group of 4 to sum. But the group of 4 might not be in the same warp, making shuffle harder. Alternatively, assign 4 consecutive threads in a warp to the same output. Warp has 32 threads, can handle 8 outputs. 32 warps handle 256 outputs... but we only have 64 outputs. So 8 warps (256 threads) can handle 64 outputs with 4 threads per output. Within each warp of 32 threads, assign groups of 4 threads to 8 outputs. For output d (handled by threads 4d, 4d+1, 4d+2, 4d+3): Thread 4d+i handles elements k = 4*warp_thread_idx_in_group + ... wait. Let me think: warp threads 0,1,2,3 handle output 0. No, k=0..255 Thread 1: k=256..511 Thread 2: k=512..767 Thread 3: k=768..1023 Each computes partial = sum outer[k] * W_out[k, d] Then reduce using __shfl_down_sync with mask=0xF (first 4 threads). Since all 4 threads are in the same warp, this works. 32 threads / 4 = 8 outputs per warp. 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps. Total outputs = 8 * 8 = 64. Perfect! This is a very clean design. For the TriMulOut kernel: Input pair: (B, N, N, pair_dim) Output: updated pair with residual. Steps: 1. LayerNorm on pair (per (b, i, j)) 2. a = pa(x) * sigmoid(ga(x)) b = pb(x) * sigmoid(gb(x)) 3. o = einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) This is: for each (b, i, j, h): o = sum_k a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h] For fixed (b, h), this is: A[b,:,h] @ B[b,:,h].T where A is N x N matrix... wait. Looking at the einsum: "bikh,bjkh->bijh" a: (B, N, N, H) - indices b, i, k, h b: (B, N, N, H) - indices b, j, k, h o: (B, N, N, H) - indices b, i, j, h So o[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h] For each (b, h), this is matrix multiplication: A_h[b] has shape (N, N), B_h[b] has shape (N, N) o[b,:,:,h] = A_h[b] @ B_h[b].T Because (A @ B.T)[i,j] = sum_k A[i,k] * B[j,k]. Yes! So for each (b, h), we do N x N @ N x N.T = N x N. B=2, H=32, so 64 such matmuls, each 32x32. 4. o = out(norm_out(o)) + bias 5. gate = sigmoid(gate_out(pair)) 6. pair_out = pair_in + gate * o For this kernel, I can: - Precompute a, b in a fast kernel, or do it on the fly - Compute the einsum as batched matrix multiply - Then apply the rest But again, fusing is key. **TriMul kernel design:** - Grid: (B, H) blocks, or (B, N, N) blocks - If grid is (B, H) with each block handling one (b, h): - Then within block, compute the full N x N = 32 x 32 = 1024 outputs for that (b, h) - But each output (i, j) needs: - a[b,i,0:N,h] and b[b,j,0:N,h] for the dot product over k - Plus the gate and pair values Block size: 1024 threads = 32x32, each handles one (i, j) Each thread (i, j): 1. Load pair[b, i, j, :] and maybe neighbors for LN? Wait, LN is per (b, i, j) on pair[b, i, j, :]. And we need pair[b, i, j, :] for the gate too. So each thread can load pair[b, i, j, :] (64 floats) into registers. Compute LN, store in registers or SMEM. 2. Compute a_i = pa(LN) * sigmoid(ga(LN)) Compute b_j = pb(LN) * sigmoid(gb(LN)) These are 32-dim vectors. But pa, ga, pb, gb are pair_dim -> hidden linears, where pair_dim=64, hidden=32. Each thread just does 2 * 32 dot products of length 64. Wait, we need a for all k, and b for all k. For o[i,j] = sum_k a[i,k] * b[j,k], each thread needs a[i,:] and b[j,:]? No: a[b,i,k,h] for all k, and b[b,j,k,h] for all k. For fixed (b, h), a is (N, N) matrix and b is (N, N) matrix. o[i,j] = sum_k A[i,k] * B[j,k] Thread (i, j) needs row i of A and row j of B, both length N=32. But to get A[i,k], we need pair[b,i,k,:] to compute the linear! Thread (i, j) only has pair[b,i,j,:] in its registers. It doesn't have pair[b,i,k,:] for k != j. So the LN and linear projections must be done before we can compute the einsum, and the results must be shared. Actually, for the einsum o[i,j] = sum_k A[i,k] * B[j,k]: - We need A[i,0:N] and B[j,0:N] - A[i,k] depends on pair[b,i,k,:] - B[j,k] depends on pair[b,j,k,:] So each thread (i,j) only needs row i of A and row j of B. But row i of A requires pair[b,i,0:N,:], and row j of B requires pair[b,j,0:N,:]. With a 32x32 block of threads, if thread (i,j) only loads pair[b,i,j,:], then to get all of row i, we need the threads with same i but different j to share their pair data. Actually, for A[i,k], thread (i,k) computes it. Then thread (i,j) needs A[i,0:N]. Similarly, thread (i,j) needs B[j,0:N], which is computed by threads (0:N, j). So in a 32x32 block: - All threads load their pair[b,i,j,:] - All threads compute LN on their pair - All threads compute A[i,j] and B[i,j] from their LN-ed pair - Now we need to transpose / share: Thread (i,j) needs A[i,0:N] and B[j,0:N] - We can write A and B to SMEM as [N][N] matrices. - Then read A[i,k] and B[j,k] for the dot product. This works! And N=32, so A SMEM = 32*32 = 1024 floats = 4KB per matrix. We need A and B SMEM: 8KB. Pair data in registers or SMEM: 32*32*64 = 64KB... that's a lot. Actually, pair is pair_dim=64. If we load it into registers (each thread holds 64 floats = 256 bytes), for 1024 threads that's 256KB... too much for registers. Better: don't keep all pair in registers. Each thread loads pair[b,i,j,:], computes LN + linears to get A[i,j] and B[i,j], then stores A[i,j] and B[i,j] to SMEM. Then reload pair[b,i,j,:] for the gate computation later. Or: since each thread only needs A[i,0:N] and B[j,0:N] for the einsum, and A and B are in SMEM, we can compute the einsum. Then later, each thread reloads pair[b,i,j,:] to compute gate. So the flow for one (b, h) block: 1. Thread (i,j) loads pair[b,i,j,:] (64 floats) 2. Computes LN, then A[i,j] and B[i,j] (each is 1 float for this h) 3. Stores A[i,j], B[i,j] to SMEM 4. __syncthreads() 5. Thread (i,j) reads A[i,0:N] and B[j,0:N] from SMEM, computes dot product = o[i,j] 6. Computes norm_out(o[i,j]), then out_linear to get o_linear[i,j] (1 float for this h) 7. Reloads pair[b,i,j,:], computes gate = sigmoid(gate_out(pair)) 8. pair_out[b,i,j,:] = pair_in[b,i,j,:] + gate[:] * o_linear But wait, o_linear is just 1 float per (i,j,h). The gate is pair_dim=64. And the residual add is pair_dim=64. Hmm, but if each block handles one h, then for step 8, each thread needs to update 64 elements of pair_out. And pair_out is pair_dim, not just 1 element. Let me re-examine. The output o from the einsum has shape (B, N, N, H). Then `o = self.out(self.norm_out(o))`. Here, `self.norm_out` is LayerNorm(hidden) where hidden=H=32. And `self.out` is Linear(hidden, pair_dim=64). But `o` is (B, N, N, H). `norm_out` applies over the last dimension (H=32) for each (b,i,j). Then `out` maps H=32 to pair_dim=64. Wait, actually in the reference code: ```python o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) o = self.out(self.norm_out(o)) return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o ``` `o` after einsum is (B, N, N, H). `self.norm_out(o)` applies LayerNorm over H for each (b,i,j). `self.out` maps from H to pair_dim, applied independently to each (b,i,j). So after out, o has shape (B, N, N, pair_dim). Then `gate = sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair))` has shape (B, N, N, pair_dim). And the final output is `gate * o` where o is now (B, N, N, pair_dim). But wait, in the return statement: `return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o` Hmm, I need to re-check the shapes. `self.gate_out` is nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim), so it maps pair to pair_dim. But in the `Model.forward`: `pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr)` So the residual is added outside. OK, so for TriMulOut kernel, if we fuse the whole thing: - We need to output tri_mul_result (B, N, N, pair_dim), not the residual-added version. - Or we can include the residual add in the kernel. For the einsum + linears, one block per (b, i, j) might be better. Block size: H=32 threads. Each block (b, i, j) computes: 1. Load pair[b,i,j,:] 2. Compute LN, then for each h in [0,H): - Compute a_h = pa(LN)[h] * sigmoid(ga(LN)[h]) - Compute b_h = pb(LN)[h] * sigmoid(gb(LN)[h]) But wait, pa, ga, pb, gb are pair_dim -> H. So thread h loads pair[b,i,k,:]?! But pair is 64-dim. Best to load pair_ik into SMEM (64 floats = 256 bytes). All threads can read it. Then all threads compute LN(pair_ik) and then their a_ik[h]. SMEM per block: - pair_ik buf: 64 floats - pair_jk buf: 64 floats - a_ik buf: 32 floats (for all h) - b_jk buf: 32 floats (for all h) - Total: ~768 bytes, very small. For the reduction: after computing a_ik and b_jk for a particular k, each thread h accumulates: accum[h] += a_ik[h] * b_jk[h] Then after k loop, accum[h] = o[b,i,j,h]. Then: - Compute norm_out on o[b,i,j,:] (32-dim vector, per-thread? All 32 threads have 1 element each). LayerNorm requires mean and var over h=0..31. Need block reduction. - Apply out linear: pair_dim=64 outputs, each is dot with o_norm and W_out[:,d]. With 32 threads, each thread can compute 2 outputs. Then compute gate[b,i,j,:] from pair[b,i,j,:]: - gate_out is Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim) - Each thread can compute 2 outputs - sigmoid - Final: pair_out[b,i,j,d] = pair_in[b,i,j,d] + gate[d] * o_linear[d] This is a lot of work but very fused. Grid: B * N * N = 2048 blocks Block: 32 threads Per block: - Loop k=0..31: load pair_ik (64), pair_jk (64), compute LN, compute a_ik[32], b_jk[32], accumulate - Then LN on accum[32] - Linear 32 -> 64 - Gate linear 64 -> 64, sigmoid - Output 64 elements Total threads: 2048 * 32 = 65536 On H100 with many SMs, this should run well. Each block does ~32*(64*2 + 32*2) + 32*64*2 + 64*64 = ~14K operations. Total ~29M operations. Small but well-fused. Actually, the biggest win is avoiding the many intermediate tensor allocations and kernel launches. Let me also think about the attention kernel more carefully. ### Attention Kernel Detail Given the model structure: ```python msa_repr = msa_repr + self.msa_attn(msa_repr) ``` `MSARowSelfAttention` does: 1. LN on msa 2. Q, K, V = linear(LN) 3. Reshape to (B, S, H, N, d) 4. attn = Q @ K.T / sqrt(d) 5. softmax over N 6. out = attn @ V 7. Permute and reshape to (B, S, N, D) 8. out linear For this, I'll use one block per (b, s), with 128 threads (4 warps x 32 threads). SMEM layout: - qkv_buffer: 3 * N * D floats = 3 * 32 * 64 * 4 = 24KB - Or we can reuse. Since we need Q, K, V simultaneously for attention, we need 3 buffers. Actually, another approach: - q_buf: 32 x 64 = 8KB - k_buf: 32 x 64 = 8KB - v_buf: 32 x 64 = 8KB - out_buf: 32 x 64 = 8KB (for attention output before linear) Total: 32KB. OK. Per thread (n, h) where n in [0,31], h in [0,3]: 1. Load X = msa[b, s, n, :] (64 floats) from global memory 2. Compute mean, var of X over 64 elements (independent per thread? Column 0: elements at 0, 1, 2, ..., 31 Column 1: elements at 1024, 1025, ..., 1055 Column 2: elements at 2048, ... Wait, W_out is stored as [64][1024] in memory, row-major. Column 0: W_out[0,0], W_out[1,0], W_out[2,0], ...? No. Row-major: W_out[d, k] where d is the first dimension. In memory: W_out[0,0], W_out[0,1], W_out[0,2], ... W_out[0,1023], W_out[1,0], W_out[1,1], ... So the "rows" (first index d) are contiguous! Thread d reads W_out[d, 0], W_out[d, 1], ... W_out[d, 0] is at base + d * 1024 + 0. Thread 0 reads base + 0. Thread 1 reads base + 1024. Thread 2 reads base + 2048. These are separated by 1024 * 4 = 4096 bytes = 4KB. A cache line is 128 bytes (32 floats). W_out[d, 0:31] is at base + d * 1024 + [0:31], which spans 128 bytes. Thread d's first read is to base + d * 4096. (Wait, 1024 floats = 4096 bytes). Since 4096 / 128 = 32, each thread reads a different cache line. Warp issues 32 cache line requests. This means 32x bandwidth waste for the first element of each column. But subsequent elements (k=1,2,...) are in the same cache line until k=32. So for k=0..31, threads are reading from cache lines they've already loaded. Actually, when thread d reads W_out[d, 0], the GPU loads the cache line containing W_out[d, 0:31]. Then when thread d reads W_out[d, 1], it's a cache hit (L1). So the warp issues 32 cache line requests for the first access (k=0), and then 0 requests for k=1..31. Then for k=32: another 32 cache line requests, and so on. Total cache line requests per thread: 1024 / 32 = 32 cache lines. Per warp: 32 * 32 = 1024 cache line requests. Per block (2 warps): ~2048 cache line requests. For 2048 blocks: ~4M cache line requests. Each cache line is 128 bytes, so ~512MB of L2 bandwidth. H100 has 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth. The effective L2 bandwidth is much higher. This should be fine. So I'll stick with 64 threads, each computing one output. Now, let me write all the kernels. Correctly handling the einsums One issue: in `load_inline`, I need to list the host wrapper functions in `functions=[...]`. Let me write the CUDA code carefully. For the MSA attention kernel: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ msa, float* __restrict__ out_msa, int B, int S, int N, int D, int H, int d, const float* __restrict__ w_q, const float* __restrict__ w_k, const float* __restrict__ w_v, const float* __restrict__ w_out, const float* __restrict__ b_out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float eps ) { int bs = blockIdx.x; int b = bs / S; int s = bs % S; int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = tid / H; // 0..31 int h = tid % H; // 0..3 // Each block processes one (b, s) // Shared memory: // q_buf: N * D // k_buf: N * D // v_buf: N * D // attn_out: N * D extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* q_buf = smem; float* k_buf = smem + N * D; float* v_buf = smem + 2 * N * D; float* out_buf = smem + 3 * N * D; // Load msa[b, s, n, :] and compute LN const float* x_ptr = msa + ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D; float x[64]; // D=64 #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { x[i] = x_ptr[i]; } // Compute mean float mean = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= D; // Compute var float var = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { x[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std; } // Compute Q, K, V for this (n, h) // Q[n, h*16:(h+1)*16] = x @ W_q[:, h*16:(h+1)*16] float q[16], k[16], v[16]; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { float q_sum = 0, k_sum = 0, v_sum = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { q_sum += x[i] * w_q[i * D + h * d + k_idx]; // Wait... } } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about weight layout. W_q is nn.Linear(D, D) with no bias. Weight shape is (D, D). In PyTorch: weight[out_feature, in_feature] = weight[D, D]. So W_q[row, col] where row is output, col is input. q[b,s,n,row] = sum_col x[b,s,n,col] * W_q[row, col] Wait, that's not right. In matrix multiply: output = input @ W.T if W is (out, in) Actually, PyTorch linear: y = x @ W^T Where x is (..., in), W is (out, in). So y[d_out] = sum_din x[d_in] * W[d_out, d_in] So W_q has shape (64, 64). W_q[d_out, d_in]. For head h and index k: d_out = h * d + k. W_q[h*d + k, d_in] So in memory: W_q[(h*d + k) * D + d_in] Similarly for output linear: out_linear[d_out] = sum_din attn_out[d_in] * W_out[d_out, d_in] W_out shape is (64, 64). Let me fix the indexing. For Q computation: ```cpp for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { int out_dim = h * d + k_idx; float q_sum = 0; for (int in_dim = 0; in_dim < D; in_dim++) { q_sum += x[in_dim] * w_q[out_dim * D + in_dim]; } q[k_idx] = q_sum; } ``` Same for K and V. Now, writing to SMEM: q_buf[n * D + h * d + k_idx] = q[k_idx] Then after __syncthreads(), compute attention. For attention, thread (n, h): ```cpp float q_local[16]; for (int k = 0; k < d; k++) q_local[k] = q_buf[n * D + h * d + k]; // Compute scores with all m float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e30f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float s = 0; for (int k = 0; k < d; k++) { s += q_local[k] * k_buf[m * D + h * d + k]; } s *= 1.0f / sqrtf((float)d); scores[m] = s; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[m]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) scores[m] *= inv_sum; // Compute output float out_local[16]; for (int k = 0; k < d; k++) out_local[k] = 0; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float attn_w = scores[m]; for (int k = 0; k < d; k++) { out_local[k] += attn_w * v_buf[m * D + h * d + k]; } } // Write to out_buf for (int k = 0; k < d; k++) { out_buf[n * D + h * d + k] = out_local[k]; } ``` Then __syncthreads(). Output linear: Thread (n, h) computes: For k_idx = 0 to d-1: int out_dim = h * d + k_idx; float sum = 0; for (int in_dim = 0; in_dim < D; in_dim++) { sum += out_buf[n * D + in_dim] * w_out[out_dim * D + in_dim]; } sum += b_out[out_dim]; sum += msa[... + out_dim]; // residual out_msa[... + out_dim] = sum; ``` Wait, each thread reads out_buf[n * D + in_dim] for in_dim = 0..63. This is reading 64 floats per thread. For 128 threads, all reading from SMEM. Since n varies by thread, different threads read different n. Threads with same n but different h read the same out_buf[n*D : n*D+64]. This means for a given n, 4 threads (h=0,1,2,3) all read the same 64 floats simultaneously. SMEM can broadcast! No bank conflicts when multiple threads read the same address. But when they read different addresses within the same 64-float range: Thread (n, h) reads out_buf[n*D + in_dim] as in_dim varies. At a given time, all threads might be reading in_dim=0, which is out_buf[n*D]. Threads with different n read different n*D addresses. Bank = (n*D + in_dim) % 32. n=0: bank 0, n=1: bank 1 (since D=64, n*D mod 32 = 0 for all n). Wait, 64 mod 32 = 0. So n*D mod 32 = 0 for all n! This means when thread (n, h) reads out_buf[n*D + in_dim], the address is bank (in_dim % 32). If all threads read the same in_dim, they all hit the same bank. Conflict. But threads with different h read different out_dim: Thread (n, h) reads out_buf[n*D + 0], out_buf[n*D + 1], ..., out_buf[n*D + 63]. For in_dim=0: all 128 threads read bank 0. 128-way conflict. Bad. How to fix? Pad the SMEM layout. Instead of out_buf[n * D + d], use out_buf[n * (D + pad) + d] where pad makes it not divisible by 32. D + 1 = 65. Bank = (n * 65 + d) % 32. n=0: d mod 32 n=1: 1 + d mod 32 n=2: 2 + d mod 32 Good! When threads read the same d but different n, they access different banks. But given the complexity, let me just write it directly and see if it's fast enough. ### TriMul Kernel 1: Einsum I'll write the kernel that computes o[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h]. As determined earlier: grid = (B, H), block = (N, N) = 1024 threads. ```cpp __global__ void tri_mul_einsum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair, float* __restrict__ o, int B, int N, int pair_dim, int H, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float eps, const float* __restrict__ w_pa, const float* __restrict__ b_pa, const float* __restrict__ w_pb, const float* __restrict__ b_pb, const float* __restrict__ w_ga, const float* __restrict__ b_ga, const float* __restrict__ w_gb, const float* __restrict__ b_gb ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int i = threadIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.y; // Load pair[b, i, j, :] float x[64]; const float* x_ptr = pair + ((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; d++) x[d] = x_ptr[d]; // LN float mean = 0; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; d++) mean += x[d]; mean /= pair_dim; float var = 0; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; d++) { float diff = x[d] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= pair_dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; d++) x[d] = (x[d] - mean) * inv_std; // Compute a and b for this (i, j, h) float pa_val = b_pa[h]; float ga_val = b_ga[h]; float pb_val = b_pb[h]; float gb_val = b_gb[h]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < pair_dim; d++) { pa_val += x[d] * w_pa[h * pair_dim + d]; ga_val += x[d] * w_ga[h * pair_dim + d]; pb_val += x[d] * w_pb[h * pair_dim + d]; gb_val += x[d] * w_gb[h * pair_dim + d]; } float sig_ga = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-ga_val)); float sig_gb = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gb_val)); float a_val = pa_val * sig_ga; float b_val = pb_val * sig_gb; // Write a and b to SMEM extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* a_smem = smem; // N x N float* b_smem = smem + N * N; // N x N a_smem[i * N + j] = a_val; b_smem[i * N + j] = b_val; } ``` Wait, in the PyTorch code: ```python a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x)) b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x)) o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) ``` a has shape (B, N, N, H). a[b, i, k, h] depends on pair[b, i, k, :]. b has shape (B, N, N, H). b[b, j, k, h] depends on pair[b, j, k, :]. o[b, i, j, h] = sum_k a[b, i, k, h] * b[b, j, k, h] Yes! But if the warp has fixed j, all threads access bank = (j+k) % 32, which is the SAME bank! 32-way conflict on b_smem! Hmm, let me think about how threads are grouped into warps. For a 32x32 block with threadIdx.x=i, threadIdx.y=j: Threads are ordered with x varying fastest. Warp 0: threads (0,0), (1,0), ..., (31,0) Warp 1: threads (0,1), (1,1), ..., (31,1) etc. So warp j has threads with j fixed, i varying 0..31. When reading a_smem[i*33 + k]: for fixed k, addresses are a_smem[0*33+k], a_smem[1*33+k], ..., a_smem[31*33+k]. Banks: k, k+1, ..., k+31 (mod 32). All different. No conflict. When reading b_smem[j*33 + k]: for warp j, all threads have the same j. Address: b_smem[j*33 + k]. ALL threads read the SAME address! This is broadcast! No bank conflict. Great! So (N+1) padding works perfectly here. Great, let me finalize this. For tri_mul kernel 2: ```cpp __global__ void tri_mul_out_kernel( const float* __restrict__ o, const float* __restrict__ pair_in, float* __restrict__ pair_out, int B, int N, int H, int pair_dim, const float* __restrict__ w_out_t, // (H, pair_dim), transposed from (pair_dim, H) const float* __restrict__ b_out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float eps, const float* __restrict__ w_gate_t, // (pair_dim, pair_dim), transposed const float* __restrict__ b_gate ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * N); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; int d = threadIdx.x; // 0..63 __shared__ float o_smem[32]; __shared__ float pair_smem[64]; // Load o and pair if (d < H) o_smem[d] = o[((b * N + i) * N + j) * H + d]; pair_smem[d] = pair_in[((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim + d]; __syncthreads(); // Compute mean and var of o_smem[0:H] // Only warp 0 participates (d < 32) float mean = 0, var = 0; if (d < H) { float val = o_smem[d]; mean = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { mean += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, mean, offset); } mean = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, mean, 0) / H; float diff = val - mean; var = diff * diff; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var, offset); } var = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var, 0) / H; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize if (d < H) { o_smem[d] = (o_smem[d] - mean) * inv_std; o_smem[d] = o_smem[d] * gamma[d] + beta[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute out[d] = dot(o_smem, w_out_t[:, d]) + b_out[d] // w_out_t has shape (H, pair_dim) after transpose // w_out_t[h, d] = w_out_t[h * pair_dim + d] float out_val = b_out[d]; #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { out_val += o_smem[h] * w_out_t[h * pair_dim + d]; } // Compute gate[d] = sigmoid(dot(pair_smem, w_gate_t[:, d]) + b_gate[d]) float gate_val = b_gate[d]; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < pair_dim; p++) { gate_val += pair_smem[p] * w_gate_t[p * pair_dim + d]; } gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_val)); pair_out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim + d] = pair_smem[d] + gate_val * out_val; } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the warp shuffle when only some threads participate. But all threads in warp load the same k_smem[m,h,:] simultaneously (broadcast). - After softmax, compute output o[n,h,:] by dotting with v_smem[m,h,:]. Step 6: Write o to out_smem[n, h*16 + k]. Let me write the code step by step for the attention kernel. ```cpp #define D_PAD 65 __global__ void msa_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ msa, float* __restrict__ out_msa, int B, int S, int N, int D, int H, int d, const float* __restrict__ w_q, const float* __restrict__ w_k, const float* __restrict__ w_v, const float* __restrict__ w_out, const float* __restrict__ b_out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float eps ) { int bs = blockIdx.x; int b = bs / S; int s = bs % S; int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = tid / H; // 0..31 int h = tid % H; // 0..3 // SMEM extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* q_smem = smem; float* k_smem = smem + N * D_PAD; float* v_smem = smem + 2 * N * D_PAD; float* out_smem = smem + 3 * N * D_PAD; // Load X and compute LN const float* x_ptr = msa + ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D; float x[64]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) x[i] = x_ptr[i]; float mean = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= D; float var = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { x[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std; x[i] = x[i] * gamma[i] + beta[i]; } // Compute Q, K, V float q[16], k[16], v[16]; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { int out_dim = h * d + k_idx; float q_sum = 0, k_sum = 0, v_sum = 0; #pragma unroll for (int in_dim = 0; in_dim < D; in_dim++) { q_sum += x[in_dim] * w_q[out_dim * D + in_dim]; k_sum += x[in_dim] * w_k[out_dim * D + in_dim]; v_sum += x[in_dim] * w_v[out_dim * D + in_dim]; } q[k_idx] = q_sum; k[k_idx] = k_sum; v[k_idx] = v_sum; } // Write to SMEM #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { q_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx] = q[k_idx]; k_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx] = k[k_idx]; v_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx] = v[k_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention for this (n, h) // Since H=4, d=16, and block has 128 threads, // threads with same h but different n form a warp-like group. // Actually, with 32 threads per warp, and n=0..31 for each h, // warp h consists of threads n=0..31 with that h. float q_local[16]; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { q_local[k_idx] = q_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx]; } float max_score = -1e30f; float scores[32]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float s = 0; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { s += q_local[k_idx] * k_smem[m * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx]; } s *= 1.0f / sqrtf((float)d); scores[m] = s; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } float sum_exp = 0; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[m]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) scores[m] *= inv_sum; float o_local[16]; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) o_local[k_idx] = 0; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float attn_w = scores[m]; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { o_local[k_idx] += attn_w * v_smem[m * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx]; } } #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { out_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx] = o_local[k_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Output linear // Thread (n, h_out): n=0..31, h_out=0..3 // Computes output[n, h_out*16 + k] for k=0..15 int h_out = h; float x_out[64]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) x_out[i] = out_smem[n * D_PAD + i]; float* out_ptr = out_msa + ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D; const float* in_ptr = msa + ((b * S + s) * N + n) * D; #pragma unroll for (int k_idx = 0; k_idx < d; k_idx++) { int out_dim = h_out * d + k_idx; float sum = b_out[out_dim]; #pragma unroll for (int in_dim = 0; in_dim < D; in_dim++) { sum += x_out[in_dim] * w_out[out_dim * D + in_dim]; } out_ptr[out_dim] = in_ptr[out_dim] + sum; } } ``` One issue: in the final output linear, I didn't ensure that all threads in the block are synchronized properly. But we have __syncthreads() before it, and then all threads read from out_smem (written in the previous step). Wait, threads with different n read different rows of out_smem. Threads with same n but different h_out read the same row. This is fine. But there's a potential bank conflict when writing to out_smem in the previous step: ```cpp out_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx] = o_local[k_idx]; ``` Threads with different n write to different rows (since D_PAD=65, n*65 gives different bank offsets). Threads with same n but different h write to different columns. At a given k_idx, threads with same n but h=0,1,2,3 write to: n*65 + 0, n*65 + 16, n*65 + 32, n*65 + 48. Banks: n, n+16, n, n+16 (mod 32). If n is even: banks 0, 16, 0, 16. Two threads (h=0 and h=2) both write to bank n! Conflict between h=0 and h=2. Similarly h=1 and h=3 conflict on bank n+16. So for writing out_smem, there's a 2-way bank conflict for each n. Not terrible. And there are only 4 writes per thread (k_idx=0..15 but unrolled). Similarly for reading out_smem in the output linear: `x_out[i] = out_smem[n * D_PAD + i];` All threads with the same n read the same address. Broadcast. Threads with different n read different rows. `n * 65 + i` mod 32 = (n + i) mod 32. For fixed i, different n read different banks. No conflict. Good. Wait, I realized the warp layout is important. In CUDA, threadIdx.x goes 0..127. Threads 0..31: warp 0. Threads 32..63: warp 1. Threads 64..95: warp 2. Threads 96..127: warp 3. And n = tid / H = tid / 4. h = tid % 4. Warp 0: tid 0..31. n = 0..7 (each repeated 4 times). h = 0,1,2,3 for each n. Wait: tid=0: n=0, h=0 tid=1: n=0, h=1 tid=2: n=0, h=2 tid=3: n=0, h=3 tid=4: n=1, h=0 ... tid=31: n=7, h=3 So warp 0 handles n=0..7, all heads. In the attention computation step, warp h processes all n for that h. But warp 0 has h=0,1,2,3 mixed together. Wait! All threads in a warp execute the same instruction stream. In the attention step, each thread computes scores for its own n and h. The code is the same for all threads. There's no warp-level divergence. Threads with different n just have different q_local and compute different scores. Threads with different h read different parts of k_smem. But when all threads in warp 0 read `k_smem[m * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx]`, they have DIFFERENT h values. k_idx is the same in the inner loop. Address for tid=0 (h=0): m*65 + 0 + 0 = m*65 tid=1 (h=1): m*65 + 16 tid=2 (h=2): m*65 + 32 tid=3 (h=3): m*65 + 48 tid=4 (h=0): m*65 + 0 Banks: m*65 mod 32 = m, m+16, m, m+16 (for tid 0,1,2,3). Wait, m*65 mod 32 = m mod 32. h*d = h*16. tid=0: bank = m + 0 = m tid=1: bank = m + 16 tid=2: bank = m + 0 tid=3: bank = m + 16 tid=4: bank = m + 0 (for n=1!) Since n=tid/4, the row is n*65. n*65 mod 32 = n mod 32. tid=0 (n=0): bank = 0 + m tid=1 (n=0): bank = 0 + m + 16 = m + 16 tid=2 (n=0): bank = m tid=3 (n=0): bank = m + 16 tid=4 (n=1): bank = 1 + m ... tid=31 (n=7, h=3): bank = 7 + m + 16 = m + 23 At fixed m: threads 0 and 2 both access bank m. 2-way conflict. Thread 1 and 3 both access bank m+16. 2-way conflict. For n=1: threads 4,5,6,7. Threads 4 and 6 both access bank m+1. So in each 4-thread group (same n), there are 2-way conflicts. Across the 32-thread warp, there are 16 threads hitting bank m (even n, h=0 or 2) and 16 threads hitting banks m+16 (even n, h=1 or 3? Wait). Actually, let me categorize: For even n: h=0: bank = n + m h=2: bank = n + m + 32 = n + m (mod 32) Same bank! h=1: bank = n + m + 16 h=3: bank = n + m + 48 = n + m + 16 (mod 32) Same bank! For odd n: h=0: bank = n + m h=2: bank = n + m h=1: bank = n + m + 16 h=3: bank = n + m + 16 In warp 0 (n=0..7): At bank m: threads with even n and h=0 or 2. That's 4 n values * 2 h values = 8 threads. At bank m+16: threads with even n and h=1 or 3. Another 8 threads? Wait, for n=0: bank = m (h=0,2) and m+16 (h=1,3). D_PAD should satisfy: for any n1 != n2, and any h1, h2: (n1 * D_PAD + h1*16 + k) % 32 != (n2 * D_PAD + h2*16 + k) % 32 when threads within a warp access simultaneously. With D_PAD = 65: n*65 mod 32 = n mod 32. Threads in warp have n=0..7. So banks are n + offset. For n=0, h=0: k n=0, h=1: k+16 n=0, h=2: k Still conflict. The conflict is fundamentally because h=0 and h=2 have the same offset mod 32 (0), and h=1 and h=3 both have 16. Let me use this. ```cpp #define D_HEAD 16 #define Q_STRIDE 17 extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: q0, q1, q2, q3, k0, k1, k2, k3, v0, v1, v2, v3, o0, o1, o2, o3 float* q_arr[4]; float* k_arr[4]; float* v_arr[4]; float* o_arr[4]; for (int h_idx = 0; h_idx < H; h_idx++) { q_arr[h_idx] = smem + h_idx * N * Q_STRIDE; k_arr[h_idx] = smem + (4 + h_idx) * N * Q_STRIDE; v_arr[h_idx] = smem + (8 + h_idx) * N * Q_STRIDE; o_arr[h_idx] = smem + (12 + h_idx) * N * Q_STRIDE; } // Write: q_arr[h][n * Q_STRIDE + k_idx] = q[k_idx]; // Read: q_local[k_idx] = q_arr[h][n * Q_STRIDE + k_idx]; s += q_local[k_idx] * k_arr[h][m * Q_STRIDE + k_idx]; ``` Now, for fixed h and m, all 32 threads (different n) read: k_arr[h][m * Q_STRIDE + k_idx] Same address! Broadcast. No conflict. And q_arr[h][n * Q_STRIDE + k_idx]: n=0..7 in one warp. Addresses: n*17 + k_idx. Banks: n*17 + k_idx mod 32 = n + k_idx mod 32. Actually, let me see if I can improve the original layout: Use D_PAD = 66. n * 66 mod 32 = n * 2 mod 32. h * 16 mod 32 = 0, 16, 0, 16. Bank = n*2 + h*16 + k. n=0, h=0: k n=0, h=1: k+16 n=0, h=2: k n=0, h=3: k+16 n=1, h=0: k+2 n=1, h=1: k+18 etc. Still h=0 and h=2 have the same bank. The conflict comes from h*16 mod 32 having period 2. Since d=16, and 2*d=32, heads h and h+2 have their columns spaced by 32, which is exactly the number of banks. There's no way around this with simple row padding. But we can pad per-head: q_smem[n * (D + 16) + h * 16 + k_idx]. No, that's the same. Or interleave within the row: q_smem[n * D + k_idx * H + h]? No, that changes the layout requirement. Actually, if we store as q_smem[n * D_PAD + h * d + k_idx], the bank is n*D_PAD + h*d + k_idx. To avoid h=0 and h=2 having the same bank mod 32, we need: 0*d mod 32 != 2*d mod 32 0 != 32 mod 32 = 0 But 2*16 = 32 ≡ 0 mod 32. It's impossible unless d is not a multiple of 16 or we adjust the head offset. If we use D_PAD such that h*d doesn't alias: We need h1*d + n*D_PAD ≠ h2*d + n*D_PAD (mod 32) for h1 ≠ h2. Let me fix this! ```cpp int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = tid / N; // 0..3 for tid=0..127 int n = tid % N; // 0..31 ``` Warp 0 (tid 0..31): h=0, n=0..31. Warp 1 (tid 32..63): h=1, n=0..31. For reading out_smem in output linear: This is the tricky part. Thread (n, h) previously corresponded to tid = h*N + n. In the output linear, we need to compute output[n, :] for all d. We have 4 warps. How do we assign output dimensions to threads? We could restructure: in the output linear, we don't care about which warp handles which head. Let's do this for tri_mul kernel 2: 128 threads per block, handling 2 (i,j) pairs. ```cpp __global__ void tri_mul_out_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * (N / 2)); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * (N / 2)); int i = rem / (N / 2); int j0 = (rem % (N / 2)) * 2; int tid = threadIdx.x; int pair_idx = tid / 64; // 0 or 1 int d = tid % 64; int j = j0 + pair_idx; if (j >= N) return; __shared__ float o_smem[2][32]; __shared__ float pair_smem[2][64]; __shared__ float mean_smem[2]; __shared__ float inv_std_smem[2]; // Load o and pair if (d < H) o_smem[pair_idx][d] = o[((b * N + i) * N + j) * H + d]; pair_smem[pair_idx][d] = pair_in[((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim + d]; __syncthreads(); // Compute LN on o_smem[pair_idx] if (d < H) { float val = o_smem[pair_idx][d]; float sum = val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); if (d == 0) mean_smem[pair_idx] = sum / H; float mean = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, mean_smem[pair_idx], 0); float diff = val - mean; float var = diff * diff; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) var += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var, offset); if (d == 0) inv_std_smem[pair_idx] = rsqrtf(var / H + eps); float inv_std = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, inv_std_smem[pair_idx], 0); o_smem[pair_idx][d] = (o_smem[pair_idx][d] - mean) * inv_std; o_smem[pair_idx][d] = o_smem[pair_idx][d] * gamma[d] + beta[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute output linear and gate float out_val = b_out[d]; #pragma unroll for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { out_val += o_smem[pair_idx][h] * w_out_t[h * pair_dim + d]; } float gate_val = b_gate[d]; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < pair_dim; p++) { gate_val += pair_smem[pair_idx][p] * w_gate_t[p * pair_dim + d]; } gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_val)); pair_out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * pair_dim + d] = pair_smem[pair_idx][d] + gate_val * out_val; } ``` But __shfl_sync within the warp only works if all participating threads pass the same arguments. In the code above, threads with d < H participate in the shuffle, others don't. But __shfl_sync requires the `mask` to include all threads in the warp that should participate. And all active threads must pass the same mask. With 128 threads = 4 warps. Warp 0 (d=0..31): all d < H=32. Good. Warp 1 (d=32..63): d >= H, so they don't enter the `if (d < H)` block. They won't call __shfl_sync, which is fine since they're not in the mask. But wait, `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, ...)` includes all threads in the warp. If warp 1 threads don't call it, will the warp hang? CUDA docs: "All threads in the warp must execute the same __shfl_sync() instruction." So if some threads in the warp are in the `if` block and call __shfl_sync, but others aren't and don't call it, that's divergence and causes undefined behavior or deadlock. To fix this, all threads in each warp must call __shfl_sync with the same mask. Since H=32, and we have 64 threads for each pair_idx? No. Thread layout: pair_idx = tid / 64, d = tid % 64. pair_idx=0: tid 0..63. pair_idx=1: tid 64..127. For pair_idx=0: tid 0..31: d=0..31, d < H (32). Good. tid 32..63: d=32..63, d >= H. Bad for __shfl_sync. So warp 1 (tid 32..63) won't enter the `if` block and won't call __shfl_sync. But warp 0 does call it. Since different warps don't need to synchronize with each other, this is fine! The requirement "all threads in the warp must execute the same __shfl_sync instruction" means within a single warp, all 32 threads must either execute it or not. Warp 0 (tid 0..31) all have d < H, so they all enter the `if` and all call __shfl_sync. Good. Warp 1 (tid 32..63) all have d >= H, so they all skip the `if`. Good. So it's OK! The two warps are independent. But wait, what about __shfl_sync with mask 0xFFFFFFFF in warp 0? `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, mean_smem[pair_idx], 0)` All threads in warp 0 participate. Good. However, `mean_smem[pair_idx]` is in shared memory. Thread d=0 writes to it. Other threads read via __shfl_sync. This works. One issue: thread d=0 writes `mean_smem[pair_idx]`. Then other threads in the warp call `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, mean_smem[pair_idx], 0)`. But __shfl_sync reads from a register, not SMEM! So `mean_smem[pair_idx]` is read by thread 0 into a register, then broadcast to other threads. This is fine because thread 0 loads it from SMEM into a register (implicitly), then __shfl_sync broadcasts it. Actually, `__shfl_sync(mask, var, srcLane)` returns the value of `var` from `srcLane`. Thread 0's `var` argument is `mean_smem[pair_idx]`, which it loads from SMEM. Other threads' `var` argument is also `mean_smem[pair_idx]`, loaded from SMEM. But they return thread 0's value. This is correct. Though it means all threads read from SMEM unnecessarily. A better approach: only thread 0 loads from SMEM into a local variable, then __shfl_sync. But the compiler might optimize this anyway. Similar for `inv_std_smem[pair_idx]`. OK, this all works. Let me now code this up properly. For the tri_mul einsum kernel, since grid is only 64 blocks, should I also try to use multiple blocks per (b,h)? For example, each block handles a tile of (i,j) pairs. Tile size 16x16 = 256 pairs. Then for N=32, we'd have 4 tiles. Grid: B * H * 4 = 256 blocks. Block: 256 threads. This increases the grid and might help occupancy. But the current design of one block per (b,h) with 1024 threads is already reasonable. 64 blocks * 1024 threads = 65536 threads. H100 SMs can handle this. The kernel is compute-bound anyway. reasoning
The problem: when using `cuda_sources` with `cpp_sources=""`, the cpp wrapper doesn't see the CUDA declarations. I need to put function declarations in the cpp source and CUDA implementation in cuda_source. Let me fix this by providing proper `.cpp` with forward declarations and `.cu` with implementations.

Make sure all the math is correct Actually, there's another issue: in my MSA attention kernel, each thread only handles `d` out of `D` output elements. If there are 4 heads and head_dim=16, then each thread handles 16 elements, and together 128 threads handle all 64 dimensions. That's correct. But wait, in the current design, all threads write to the same output memory for overlapping dimensions. Since each head handles disjoint dimension ranges and each thread within a head handles the same head, there shouldn't be conflicts. But the `h` is computed as `tid / N` and `n = tid % N`. So for 128 threads (N=32, H=4): - tid=0: h=0, n=0 - tid=1: h=0, n=1 ... - tid=31: h=0, n=31 - tid=32: h=1, n=0 ... Each thread handles `d=16` dimensions (the head's slices). So tid=0 writes to out_dim=0..15 for n=0. tid=1 writes to out_dim=0..15 for n=1. No conflicts. Good. Let me just fix the compilation issue with proper flags and resubmit.
